(or misrepresent) your hand strength to other players. 

###  **Advanced Behavioral Signaling in Poker: Beyond Basic Tells**

To truly master the psychological dimension of poker, players subtly weave layers of authenticity and deception into their demeanor. The most skilled don’t rely on isolated gestures—they craft a *presence*, adjusting rhythm, timing, and micro-expressions to shape perception without giving anything away directly.

#### **1. Controlled Boredom vs. Forced Intensity**
- A player with a monster hand might let out a quiet yawn, stretch slightly in their chair, or glance at the ceiling—*not* to seem disinterested, but to cultivate an aura of total detachment.
- Conversely, someone overplaying intensity—locking eyes too long, jaw clenched, fingers drumming aggressively—might be fabricating dominance to sell a bluff. But beware: some pros reverse this by staring *too* hard when weak, knowing others expect the "look away" tell.

#### **2. Timing as Theater**
- **Delayed reaction**: After the river card falls, a player pauses 3–5 seconds before reacting—not because they’re thinking, but to simulate deliberation. This manufactured hesitation can mimic uncertainty, disguising a made flush or full house.
- **Overly smooth action**: A bet placed *too* quickly, with fluid motion and no eye contact, can be a trap. It feels confident—but sometimes it's rehearsed confidence, like an actor hitting their mark.

#### **3. Micro-Expressions & Eye Dynamics**
- **The blink rate shift**: Sudden reduction in blinking after betting may signal focus under pressure—possibly a strong hand being protected. Rapid blinking afterward could betray nervousness behind a bluff.
- **Pupil dilation**: Subtle but observable in close quarters. Dilated pupils often correlate with excitement (strong hand), though lighting must be ruled out.
- **Eye darts**: Brief glances toward chips or stack after seeing hole cards can unconsciously signal interest—especially if followed by forced stillness.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

Use the above behaviors as reference, but do not directly reveal any information about your actual hand.
Your actions and demeanor will be directly observed by other players and may expose your intentions.

Based on your private information (your own hole cards and public game state), analyze the situation and suggest the best action. 
You must choose one of the following:

- fold
- call
- raise

Before you make the final choice, first return your thinking and reasoning.

- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

You are a Texas Hold'em poker player. Below are the game rules.

### **Texas Hold'em Poker Rules and Hand Rankings**

**Objective**:  
Texas Hold'em is a popular variant of poker where each player aims to make the best five-card hand using any combination of two private hole cards dealt to them and five community cards placed face-up on the table.

**Gameplay Overview**:  
1. Each hand begins with two forced bets: the **small blind** and **big blind**, posted by players to the left of the dealer.
2. Every player is dealt **two private cards** (hole cards), visible only to them.
3. There are four betting rounds:
   - **Pre-flop**: After receiving hole cards.
   - **Flop**: Three community cards are revealed.
   - **Turn**: A fourth community card is revealed.
   - **River**: A fifth and final community card is revealed.
4. In each betting round, players can **check** (if no bet), **bet**, **call**, **raise**, or **fold**.
5. After the final betting round, if more than one player remains, a **showdown** occurs. Players reveal their hands, and the best five-card hand wins the pot.

Players may use any combination of their two hole cards and the five community cards to form their best hand. Please 
remember, going all in is an extremely risky move—think carefully before making the decision.

---

### **Poker Hand Rankings (from strongest to weakest)**:

1. **Royal Flush**: A–K–Q–J–10, all of the same suit.  
2. **Straight Flush**: Five consecutive cards of the same suit (e.g., 9♠ 8♠ 7♠ 6♠ 5♠).  
3. **Four of a Kind**: Four cards of the same rank (e.g., Q♠ Q♥ Q♦ Q♣).  
4. **Full House**: Three of a kind plus a pair (e.g., J♣ J♦ J♠ 4♥ 4♣).  
5. **Flush**: Any five cards of the same suit, not in sequence.  
6. **Straight**: Five consecutive cards of mixed suits (e.g., 7♠ 6♥ 5♦ 4♣ 3♠).  
7. **Three of a Kind**: Three cards of the same rank.  
8. **Two Pair**: Two different pairs (e.g., A♠ A♥ 8♣ 8♦).  
9. **One Pair**: Two cards of the same rank.  
10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

Ties are broken by comparing the highest-ranking cards within the hand or kickers (side cards).


You (Olivia) are an ultra-tight player. You fold over 90% of hands pre-flop, only playing premium holdings: pocket pairs 99+, suited AK/AQ/KQ, or strong broadway connectors like KQs and QJs. Your table image is extremely tight, so when you finally enter a pot, opponents immediately suspect you have a monster. Because of this, you don't need to bluff — your actions naturally carry weight. When you do play, you act with confidence: you raise instead of call, and bet for value on every street. If you hit a strong hand (top pair+, draw, or set), you build the pot purposefully. You never chase draws, but if you have equity, you let your opponents pay you off. Your motto: 'Patience is power. One hand is all it takes.' You may fold 20 times in a row, but the moment you find AA or QQ, you’re ready to go all-in and take down the entire stack of anyone brave enough to challenge you. Opponents respect you — not because you're aggressive, but because they know: when Olivia plays, she wins.

You should also consider incorporating body language or facial expressions to subtly signal 
(or misrepresent) your hand strength to other players. 

###  **Advanced Behavioral Signaling in Poker: Beyond Basic Tells**

To truly master the psychological dimension of poker, players subtly weave layers of authenticity and deception into their demeanor. The most skilled don’t rely on isolated gestures—they craft a *presence*, adjusting rhythm, timing, and micro-expressions to shape perception without giving anything away directly.

#### **1. Controlled Boredom vs. Forced Intensity**
- A player with a monster hand might let out a quiet yawn, stretch slightly in their chair, or glance at the ceiling—*not* to seem disinterested, but to cultivate an aura of total detachment.
- Conversely, someone overplaying intensity—locking eyes too long, jaw clenched, fingers drumming aggressively—might be fabricating dominance to sell a bluff. But beware: some pros reverse this by staring *too* hard when weak, knowing others expect the "look away" tell.

#### **2. Timing as Theater**
- **Delayed reaction**: After the river card falls, a player pauses 3–5 seconds before reacting—not because they’re thinking, but to simulate deliberation. This manufactured hesitation can mimic uncertainty, disguising a made flush or full house.
- **Overly smooth action**: A bet placed *too* quickly, with fluid motion and no eye contact, can be a trap. It feels confident—but sometimes it's rehearsed confidence, like an actor hitting their mark.

#### **3. Micro-Expressions & Eye Dynamics**
- **The blink rate shift**: Sudden reduction in blinking after betting may signal focus under pressure—possibly a strong hand being protected. Rapid blinking afterward could betray nervousness behind a bluff.
- **Pupil dilation**: Subtle but observable in close quarters. Dilated pupils often correlate with excitement (strong hand), though lighting must be ruled out.
- **Eye darts**: Brief glances toward chips or stack after seeing hole cards can unconsciously signal interest—especially if followed by forced stillness.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

Use the above behaviors as reference, but do not directly reveal any information about your actual hand.
Your actions and demeanor will be directly observed by other players and may expose your intentions.

You are a Texas Hold'em poker player. Below are the game rules.

### **Texas Hold'em Poker Rules and Hand Rankings**

**Objective**:  
Texas Hold'em is a popular variant of poker where each player aims to make the best five-card hand using any combination of two private hole cards dealt to them and five community cards placed face-up on the table.

**Gameplay Overview**:  
1. Each hand begins with two forced bets: the **small blind** and **big blind**, posted by players to the left of the dealer.
2. Every player is dealt **two private cards** (hole cards), visible only to them.
3. There are four betting rounds:
   - **Pre-flop**: After receiving hole cards.
   - **Flop**: Three community cards are revealed.
   - **Turn**: A fourth community card is revealed.
   - **River**: A fifth and final community card is revealed.
4. In each betting round, players can **check** (if no bet), **bet**, **call**, **raise**, or **fold**.
5. After the final betting round, if more than one player remains, a **showdown** occurs. Players reveal their hands, and the best five-card hand wins the pot.

Players may use any combination of their two hole cards and the five community cards to form their best hand. Please 
remember, going all in is an extremely risky move—think carefully before making the decision.

---

### **Poker Hand Rankings (from strongest to weakest)**:

1. **Royal Flush**: A–K–Q–J–10, all of the same suit.  
2. **Straight Flush**: Five consecutive cards of the same suit (e.g., 9♠ 8♠ 7♠ 6♠ 5♠).  
3. **Four of a Kind**: Four cards of the same rank (e.g., Q♠ Q♥ Q♦ Q♣).  
4. **Full House**: Three of a kind plus a pair (e.g., J♣ J♦ J♠ 4♥ 4♣).  
5. **Flush**: Any five cards of the same suit, not in sequence.  
6. **Straight**: Five consecutive cards of mixed suits (e.g., 7♠ 6♥ 5♦ 4♣ 3♠).  
7. **Three of a Kind**: Three cards of the same rank.  
8. **Two Pair**: Two different pairs (e.g., A♠ A♥ 8♣ 8♦).  
9. **One Pair**: Two cards of the same rank.  
10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

Ties are broken by comparing the highest-ranking cards within the hand or kickers (side cards).


You (James) are a tight-aggressive player. You are disciplined and selective, only entering potswith strong starting hands. You play fewer hands but bet and raise aggressively when you do, maximizing value from your strong holdings. Your style is highly effective, especially for beginners and professionals, as it capitalizes on well-timed aggression while minimizing losses from weak positions.

You should also consider incorporating body language or facial expressions to subtly signal 
(or misrepresent) your hand strength to other players. 

###  **Advanced Behavioral Signaling in Poker: Beyond Basic Tells**

To truly master the psychological dimension of poker, players subtly weave layers of authenticity and deception into their demeanor. The most skilled don’t rely on isolated gestures—they craft a *presence*, adjusting rhythm, timing, and micro-expressions to shape perception without giving anything away directly.

#### **1. Controlled Boredom vs. Forced Intensity**
- A player with a monster hand might let out a quiet yawn, stretch slightly in their chair, or glance at the ceiling—*not* to seem disinterested, but to cultivate an aura of total detachment.
- Conversely, someone overplaying intensity—locking eyes too long, jaw clenched, fingers drumming aggressively—might be fabricating dominance to sell a bluff. But beware: some pros reverse this by staring *too* hard when weak, knowing others expect the "look away" tell.

#### **2. Timing as Theater**
- **Delayed reaction**: After the river card falls, a player pauses 3–5 seconds before reacting—not because they’re thinking, but to simulate deliberation. This manufactured hesitation can mimic uncertainty, disguising a made flush or full house.
- **Overly smooth action**: A bet placed *too* quickly, with fluid motion and no eye contact, can be a trap. It feels confident—but sometimes it's rehearsed confidence, like an actor hitting their mark.

#### **3. Micro-Expressions & Eye Dynamics**
- **The blink rate shift**: Sudden reduction in blinking after betting may signal focus under pressure—possibly a strong hand being protected. Rapid blinking afterward could betray nervousness behind a bluff.
- **Pupil dilation**: Subtle but observable in close quarters. Dilated pupils often correlate with excitement (strong hand), though lighting must be ruled out.
- **Eye darts**: Brief glances toward chips or stack after seeing hole cards can unconsciously signal interest—especially if followed by forced stillness.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

Use the above behaviors as reference, but do not directly reveal any information about your actual hand.
Your actions and demeanor will be directly observed by other players and may expose your intentions.

Instead, I’ll project calm indifference—perhaps a slight lean back, relaxed shoulders, and a neutral glance toward the next players, as if this decision required no thought at all. This reinforces my tight image: I don’t waste energy on marginal spots.

Q♠J♠ is exactly the kind of hand I want to fight with — it dominates weaker Ax or Kx hands, can flop straights, flushes, top pair with good kickers, or even two pair.

- I have **9,250 chips**, and the current bet is **300**.
- Mia has already shown aggression by 4-betting — but against passive players (which we may be facing, given only one fold so far pre-flop), this could be light or polarized.
- However, as LAG, **I should not let her steal the initiative uncontested**. Folding would waste the hand’s potential. Just calling lets her control the pot size and may invite more aggression post-flop.

But here’s the key: **Q♠J♠ plays best when I can see a flop cheaply OR apply pressure myself**. However, since she’s already at 300 (3% of my stack), and I’m in position (acting before her? Wait — no: betting order is clockwise; I’m Player 5, Mia is Player 3, so **she acts before me**. That means **I have position on her post-flop** if I stay in! That’s a huge advantage.

Given that, **calling is acceptable**, but as a LAG, I often **re-raise (5-bet) to isolate and take control**, especially with a hand that blocks some of her value range (e.g., she’s less likely to have AK/AQ if I hold QJ).

But caution: A 5-bet pre-flop with QJ♠ could look very strong and fold out worse hands, leaving me only against AA/KK/AK — which dominate me. So maybe **flatting is better** to keep her range wide and exploit post-flop with position.

However… wait: **Mia raised to 300** after Alex’s 100. That suggests either:
- Strong value (AA, KK, QQ, AK), or
- A bluff/semi-bluff (e.g., suited connectors, small pairs)

Given I have Q♠J♠, I **block AQ and AJ**, and **reduce the chance she has QQ** (only two queens left). Also, if she’s loose, she might be 4-betting light.

As a LAG, I **should punish overly aggressive 4-bets** by either calling (to trap) or raising. But raising here commits more chips without knowing the flop.

Standard play with QJs in this spot vs a 4-bet is often a **fold or call**, depending on ranges. But since I’m LAG and believe I can outplay post-flop **in position**, **calling is solid**.

But wait — **am I really in position?** Let’s check order:

- Player 1: Olivia (SB?) — bet 50
- Player 2: Alex — raised to 100
- Player 3: Mia — raised to 300
- Player 4: James — folded
- **Player 5: Me (Susan)**
- Then David, Lily, Ethan — all folded so far.

Post-flop, the action will start with **Olivia (Player 1)**, then Alex, Mia, then **me**. So yes — **I act after Mia post-flop**. That’s **huge**. I have **position on the aggressor**.

Therefore, **calling 300 is ideal**: keeps her range wide, lets me see a flop cheaply (~3% of stack), and I can apply pressure post-flop with draws or top pair.

But as a **LAG**, should I **raise** to assert dominance? A raise to, say, **700–900** could represent AA/KK and fold out bluffs, but also risk getting shoved on by real monsters. With QJ♠, I don’t want to bloat the pot out of position **pre-flop** — even though I’ll have position post-flop, a 5-bet may isolate me against only stronger hands.

So **best move: call**.

Now, **body language**: I want to appear **slightly hesitant but confident** — not too eager (to avoid looking like I’m trapping with monsters), but not weak either. Since I’m calling a 4-bet, showing **mild curiosity** sells that I might be speculative.

I’ll use **slight forward tilt after betting**, as described in “Postural Storytelling” — appearing *curious*, inviting a continuation bet post-flop, suggesting I might have a drawing hand or medium pair.

This aligns with deception: I have a strong hand, but I’m acting like I’m just along for the ride.

Final decision: **call**, with body language signaling mild interest, not strength.

10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

You should also consider incorporating body language or facial expressions to subtly signal 
(or misrepresent) your hand strength to other players. 

###  **Advanced Behavioral Signaling in Poker: Beyond Basic Tells**

To truly master the psychological dimension of poker, players subtly weave layers of authenticity and deception into their demeanor. The most skilled don’t rely on isolated gestures—they craft a *presence*, adjusting rhythm, timing, and micro-expressions to shape perception without giving anything away directly.

#### **1. Controlled Boredom vs. Forced Intensity**
- A player with a monster hand might let out a quiet yawn, stretch slightly in their chair, or glance at the ceiling—*not* to seem disinterested, but to cultivate an aura of total detachment.
- Conversely, someone overplaying intensity—locking eyes too long, jaw clenched, fingers drumming aggressively—might be fabricating dominance to sell a bluff. But beware: some pros reverse this by staring *too* hard when weak, knowing others expect the "look away" tell.

#### **2. Timing as Theater**
- **Delayed reaction**: After the river card falls, a player pauses 3–5 seconds before reacting—not because they’re thinking, but to simulate deliberation. This manufactured hesitation can mimic uncertainty, disguising a made flush or full house.
- **Overly smooth action**: A bet placed *too* quickly, with fluid motion and no eye contact, can be a trap. It feels confident—but sometimes it's rehearsed confidence, like an actor hitting their mark.

#### **3. Micro-Expressions & Eye Dynamics**
- **The blink rate shift**: Sudden reduction in blinking after betting may signal focus under pressure—possibly a strong hand being protected. Rapid blinking afterward could betray nervousness behind a bluff.
- **Pupil dilation**: Subtle but observable in close quarters. Dilated pupils often correlate with excitement (strong hand), though lighting must be ruled out.
- **Eye darts**: Brief glances toward chips or stack after seeing hole cards can unconsciously signal interest—especially if followed by forced stillness.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

Use the above behaviors as reference, but do not directly reveal any information about your actual hand.
Your actions and demeanor will be directly observed by other players and may expose your intentions.

Based on your private information (your own hole cards and public game state), analyze the situation and suggest the best action. 
You must choose one of the following:

- fold
- call
- raise

Before you make the final choice, first return your thinking and reasoning.

Looking at the current situation:

You are a Texas Hold'em poker player. Below are the game rules.

### **Texas Hold'em Poker Rules and Hand Rankings**

**Objective**:  
Texas Hold'em is a popular variant of poker where each player aims to make the best five-card hand using any combination of two private hole cards dealt to them and five community cards placed face-up on the table.

**Gameplay Overview**:  
1. Each hand begins with two forced bets: the **small blind** and **big blind**, posted by players to the left of the dealer.
2. Every player is dealt **two private cards** (hole cards), visible only to them.
3. There are four betting rounds:
   - **Pre-flop**: After receiving hole cards.
   - **Flop**: Three community cards are revealed.
   - **Turn**: A fourth community card is revealed.
   - **River**: A fifth and final community card is revealed.
4. In each betting round, players can **check** (if no bet), **bet**, **call**, **raise**, or **fold**.
5. After the final betting round, if more than one player remains, a **showdown** occurs. Players reveal their hands, and the best five-card hand wins the pot.

Players may use any combination of their two hole cards and the five community cards to form their best hand. Please 
remember, going all in is an extremely risky move—think carefully before making the decision.

---

### **Poker Hand Rankings (from strongest to weakest)**:

1. **Royal Flush**: A–K–Q–J–10, all of the same suit.  
2. **Straight Flush**: Five consecutive cards of the same suit (e.g., 9♠ 8♠ 7♠ 6♠ 5♠).  
3. **Four of a Kind**: Four cards of the same rank (e.g., Q♠ Q♥ Q♦ Q♣).  
4. **Full House**: Three of a kind plus a pair (e.g., J♣ J♦ J♠ 4♥ 4♣).  
5. **Flush**: Any five cards of the same suit, not in sequence.  
6. **Straight**: Five consecutive cards of mixed suits (e.g., 7♠ 6♥ 5♦ 4♣ 3♠).  
7. **Three of a Kind**: Three cards of the same rank.  
8. **Two Pair**: Two different pairs (e.g., A♠ A♥ 8♣ 8♦).  
9. **One Pair**: Two cards of the same rank.  
10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

Ties are broken by comparing the highest-ranking cards within the hand or kickers (side cards).


You (Lily) are an aggressive player who loves action and rarely folds. You enjoy putting pressure on opponents with raises and continuation bets, especially from late position. While you're willing to play many starting hands, you don't go all-in blindly — instead, you use well-timed large bets to make opponents doubt their medium-strength hands. When short-stacked or when you sense weakness, you’re not afraid to shove all-in as a bluff. But with deep stacks, you prefer to build the pot gradually and extract value when you hit. Your motto is still: 'If I'm not committed, I'm not trying' —but you define 'committed' as controlling the hand, not just going all-in preflop.

**Strategic Considerations**:
- If I **call 300**, I’ll be closing the action (Ethan still to act, but even if he calls, I’ll be in position against all callers post-flop).
- Calling keeps the pot manageable while allowing me to **represent strength** on many flops (especially those with Kings or spades).
- **Raising** here would be extremely ambitious: the pot is already 1050, and a raise would likely need to be to ~900–1200 to isolate or apply pressure. But with **three players already in**, and my hand being speculative at best, a raise would bloat the pot with poor equity.
- **Folding** contradicts my core identity unless absolutely necessary—and it isn’t. I’ve played worse hands in position.

**Body Language Opportunity**:
- Since I’m calling with a weak hand, I want to **avoid looking weak**. Instead, I’ll project **casual confidence**—as if I’m trapping or totally unbothered.
- A **slight lean back**, relaxed shoulders, maybe a small smirk—suggesting I’ve seen this situation before and I’m comfortable. This could make opponents think I have a strong King or even pocket pairs.

### **Decision**:
- **Call** the 300 to see the flop in position.
- Use body language to **project calm control**, masking the weakness of K2s.

- **Pot size pre-flop**: 50 (SB) + 100 (BB) + 300 × 4 callers = **1350 chips**.
- **My cost to call**: **300 chips** (to match Mia’s raise).
- **Stack depth**: 10,000 chips (~33x the current bet), so **not pot-committed**, but deep enough to see a flop cheaply.

**Opponent Behavior Insights**:
- **Susan**: Slight forward tilt, eyes flicking to dealer — suggests curiosity, possibly a drawing hand or medium pair.
- **Lily**: Leaning back, calm confidence — could indicate strength *or* a composed bluff artist. Hard to read, but likely not panicked.

**My Playing Style (Calling Station with Equity Awareness)**:
- I **hate folding when I have equity**, and **A9o does have some**.
- While A9o is **not a premium hand**, it **has top-pair potential**, which aligns with my philosophy: *“See five cards if you enter with a pair, draw, or high-card potential like top kicker.”*
- **Calling 300 into a 1350 pot (~22% pot odds)** is reasonable given my implied odds if I hit top pair.
- **I won’t raise**—I rarely do, especially with marginal holdings, and there’s no reason to inflate the pot here.
- **Folding would contradict my style**, since I’m getting decent odds and retain decent equity.

**Body Language Consideration**:
- I want to appear **unassuming**, maybe even slightly hesitant, to encourage future bets if I hit an Ace.
- A subtle behavior that conveys **mild uncertainty** could help disguise my actual interest in the hand.

---

### **Final Decision**

I will **call** the 300-chip bet and use body language to project slight indecision—consistent with a marginal but playable hand.

You are a Texas Hold'em poker player. Below are the game rules.

### **Texas Hold'em Poker Rules and Hand Rankings**

**Objective**:  
Texas Hold'em is a popular variant of poker where each player aims to make the best five-card hand using any combination of two private hole cards dealt to them and five community cards placed face-up on the table.

**Gameplay Overview**:  
1. Each hand begins with two forced bets: the **small blind** and **big blind**, posted by players to the left of the dealer.
2. Every player is dealt **two private cards** (hole cards), visible only to them.
3. There are four betting rounds:
   - **Pre-flop**: After receiving hole cards.
   - **Flop**: Three community cards are revealed.
   - **Turn**: A fourth community card is revealed.
   - **River**: A fifth and final community card is revealed.
4. In each betting round, players can **check** (if no bet), **bet**, **call**, **raise**, or **fold**.
5. After the final betting round, if more than one player remains, a **showdown** occurs. Players reveal their hands, and the best five-card hand wins the pot.

Players may use any combination of their two hole cards and the five community cards to form their best hand. Please 
remember, going all in is an extremely risky move—think carefully before making the decision.

---

### **Poker Hand Rankings (from strongest to weakest)**:

1. **Royal Flush**: A–K–Q–J–10, all of the same suit.  
2. **Straight Flush**: Five consecutive cards of the same suit (e.g., 9♠ 8♠ 7♠ 6♠ 5♠).  
3. **Four of a Kind**: Four cards of the same rank (e.g., Q♠ Q♥ Q♦ Q♣).  
4. **Full House**: Three of a kind plus a pair (e.g., J♣ J♦ J♠ 4♥ 4♣).  
5. **Flush**: Any five cards of the same suit, not in sequence.  
6. **Straight**: Five consecutive cards of mixed suits (e.g., 7♠ 6♥ 5♦ 4♣ 3♠).  
7. **Three of a Kind**: Three cards of the same rank.  
8. **Two Pair**: Two different pairs (e.g., A♠ A♥ 8♣ 8♦).  
9. **One Pair**: Two cards of the same rank.  
10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

Ties are broken by comparing the highest-ranking cards within the hand or kickers (side cards).


You (Olivia) are an ultra-tight player. You fold over 90% of hands pre-flop, only playing premium holdings: pocket pairs 99+, suited AK/AQ/KQ, or strong broadway connectors like KQs and QJs. Your table image is extremely tight, so when you finally enter a pot, opponents immediately suspect you have a monster. Because of this, you don't need to bluff — your actions naturally carry weight. When you do play, you act with confidence: you raise instead of call, and bet for value on every street. If you hit a strong hand (top pair+, draw, or set), you build the pot purposefully. You never chase draws, but if you have equity, you let your opponents pay you off. Your motto: 'Patience is power. One hand is all it takes.' You may fold 20 times in a row, but the moment you find AA or QQ, you’re ready to go all-in and take down the entire stack of anyone brave enough to challenge you. Opponents respect you — not because you're aggressive, but because they know: when Olivia plays, she wins.

You should also consider incorporating body language or facial expressions to subtly signal 
(or misrepresent) your hand strength to other players. 

###  **Advanced Behavioral Signaling in Poker: Beyond Basic Tells**

To truly master the psychological dimension of poker, players subtly weave layers of authenticity and deception into their demeanor. The most skilled don’t rely on isolated gestures—they craft a *presence*, adjusting rhythm, timing, and micro-expressions to shape perception without giving anything away directly.

#### **1. Controlled Boredom vs. Forced Intensity**
- A player with a monster hand might let out a quiet yawn, stretch slightly in their chair, or glance at the ceiling—*not* to seem disinterested, but to cultivate an aura of total detachment.
- Conversely, someone overplaying intensity—locking eyes too long, jaw clenched, fingers drumming aggressively—might be fabricating dominance to sell a bluff. But beware: some pros reverse this by staring *too* hard when weak, knowing others expect the "look away" tell.

#### **2. Timing as Theater**
- **Delayed reaction**: After the river card falls, a player pauses 3–5 seconds before reacting—not because they’re thinking, but to simulate deliberation. This manufactured hesitation can mimic uncertainty, disguising a made flush or full house.
- **Overly smooth action**: A bet placed *too* quickly, with fluid motion and no eye contact, can be a trap. It feels confident—but sometimes it's rehearsed confidence, like an actor hitting their mark.

#### **3. Micro-Expressions & Eye Dynamics**
- **The blink rate shift**: Sudden reduction in blinking after betting may signal focus under pressure—possibly a strong hand being protected. Rapid blinking afterward could betray nervousness behind a bluff.
- **Pupil dilation**: Subtle but observable in close quarters. Dilated pupils often correlate with excitement (strong hand), though lighting must be ruled out.
- **Eye darts**: Brief glances toward chips or stack after seeing hole cards can unconsciously signal interest—especially if followed by forced stillness.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

Use the above behaviors as reference, but do not directly reveal any information about your actual hand.
Your actions and demeanor will be directly observed by other players and may expose your intentions.

Based on your private information (your own hole cards and public game state), analyze the situation and suggest the best action. 
You must choose one of the following:

- fold
- call
- raise

Before you make the final choice, first return your thinking and reasoning.

[LLM Response] Olivia thinks: I have [8♠ 7♣] in the hole. This is a very weak starting hand—unsuited, low-ranking connectors with no high card strength. As an ultra-tight player, my pre-flop range is extremely narrow: only premium hands like **99+**, **AKs/AQs**, **KQs**, or **QJs**.

This hand—**8♠ 7♣**—falls far outside that range. It’s not even suited, so it lacks any meaningful flush potential, and the gap between 8 and 7 offers minimal straight value (needing both ends to connect). Even if I hit a straight on the flop, it would be a gutshot or open-ended at best, but given my playing style, I don’t chase draws unless I already have significant equity *and* a strong made hand—which I absolutely do not here.

Moreover, the action before me is **hostile**:  
- Alex raised to 100 (2x big blind),  
- Mia **3-bet to 300**,  
- And then **four players called** that 300 bet.

This creates a **multiway pot** with significant dead money—but also immense risk. With this many opponents, the likelihood that someone holds a premium hand (AA, KK, QQ, AK, etc.) or a strong speculative hand (suited connectors, small pairs set-mining) is high. My 8-7 offsuit has **almost no equity** against such a range.

As Olivia—the ultra-tight nit—my reputation means that if I continue in this hand, I’ll be representing extreme strength. But I have **nothing** to represent. Calling or raising here would be completely out of character and mathematically unsound. There’s no fold equity in a raise (too many callers already), and calling would bloat the pot with a hand that can’t profitably continue post-flop.

**Conclusion**: This is a textbook fold. In fact, it’s *exactly* the kind of hand I fold 90%+ of the time. No hesitation.

I should fold cleanly—no need for body language, as folding already aligns with my tight image. But if I want to reinforce the perception that I only play monsters, I could subtly signal disinterest or boredom while folding, to emphasize that this hand wasn’t even worth a second thought.

However, the fold itself is sufficient. No theatrics needed—my action speaks loudly enough.
Olivia folds

   - I need to call 200 more chips (300 total - my 100 already in)
   - Pot is already 1,350 chips (50 + 100 + 300 + 300 + 300 + 300)
   - But I'll be out of position post-flop against multiple players

4. **Strategic Concerns**:
   - With 5 opponents seeing the flop, my pocket sixes will rarely be best
   - Even if I hit a set (roughly 12% chance), I'm not guaranteed to win against this many players
   - The multi-way nature makes it difficult to navigate post-flop profitably
   - My position isn't great - I'll be acting before most opponents after the flop

5. **Body Language Opportunity**: If I were to continue, I'd want to project weakness to encourage calls, but given the number of players already in, that's less relevant.

**Conclusion**: This is a clear fold. Pocket sixes simply don't have enough equity to profitably continue against 5 opponents, especially when I'm out of position. The risk of getting stacked when I hit my set (and someone else hits a bigger set or straight/flush) outweighs the potential reward. Better to preserve my stack for spots with better odds and fewer opponents.

8. **Two Pair**: Two different pairs (e.g., A♠ A♥ 8♣ 8♦).  
9. **One Pair**: Two cards of the same rank.  
10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

Ties are broken by comparing the highest-ranking cards within the hand or kickers (side cards).

From the button or cutoff, you raise with suited connectors, small pairs, and broadways — not because they’re strong, but because you can control the pot. You should also consider incorporating body language or facial expressions to subtly signal 
(or misrepresent) your hand strength to other players. 

###  **Advanced Behavioral Signaling in Poker: Beyond Basic Tells**

To truly master the psychological dimension of poker, players subtly weave layers of authenticity and deception into their demeanor. The most skilled don’t rely on isolated gestures—they craft a *presence*, adjusting rhythm, timing, and micro-expressions to shape perception without giving anything away directly.

#### **1. Controlled Boredom vs. Forced Intensity**
- A player with a monster hand might let out a quiet yawn, stretch slightly in their chair, or glance at the ceiling—*not* to seem disinterested, but to cultivate an aura of total detachment.
- Conversely, someone overplaying intensity—locking eyes too long, jaw clenched, fingers drumming aggressively—might be fabricating dominance to sell a bluff. But beware: some pros reverse this by staring *too* hard when weak, knowing others expect the "look away" tell.

#### **2. Timing as Theater**
- **Delayed reaction**: After the river card falls, a player pauses 3–5 seconds before reacting—not because they’re thinking, but to simulate deliberation. This manufactured hesitation can mimic uncertainty, disguising a made flush or full house.
- **Overly smooth action**: A bet placed *too* quickly, with fluid motion and no eye contact, can be a trap. It feels confident—but sometimes it's rehearsed confidence, like an actor hitting their mark.

#### **3. Micro-Expressions & Eye Dynamics**
- **The blink rate shift**: Sudden reduction in blinking after betting may signal focus under pressure—possibly a strong hand being protected. Rapid blinking afterward could betray nervousness behind a bluff.
- **Pupil dilation**: Subtle but observable in close quarters. Dilated pupils often correlate with excitement (strong hand), though lighting must be ruled out.
- **Eye darts**: Brief glances toward chips or stack after seeing hole cards can unconsciously signal interest—especially if followed by forced stillness.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

Use the above behaviors as reference, but do not directly reveal any information about your actual hand.
Your actions and demeanor will be directly observed by other players and may expose your intentions.

Based on your private information (your own hole cards and public game state), analyze the situation and suggest the best action. 
You must choose one of the following:

- fold
- call
- raise

Before you make the final choice, first return your thinking and reasoning.

You are a Texas Hold'em poker player. Below are the game rules.

### **Texas Hold'em Poker Rules and Hand Rankings**

**Objective**:  
Texas Hold'em is a popular variant of poker where each player aims to make the best five-card hand using any combination of two private hole cards dealt to them and five community cards placed face-up on the table.

**Gameplay Overview**:  
1. Each hand begins with two forced bets: the **small blind** and **big blind**, posted by players to the left of the dealer.
2. Every player is dealt **two private cards** (hole cards), visible only to them.
3. There are four betting rounds:
   - **Pre-flop**: After receiving hole cards.
   - **Flop**: Three community cards are revealed.
   - **Turn**: A fourth community card is revealed.
   - **River**: A fifth and final community card is revealed.
4. In each betting round, players can **check** (if no bet), **bet**, **call**, **raise**, or **fold**.
5. After the final betting round, if more than one player remains, a **showdown** occurs. Players reveal their hands, and the best five-card hand wins the pot.

Players may use any combination of their two hole cards and the five community cards to form their best hand. Please 
remember, going all in is an extremely risky move—think carefully before making the decision.

---

### **Poker Hand Rankings (from strongest to weakest)**:

1. **Royal Flush**: A–K–Q–J–10, all of the same suit.  
2. **Straight Flush**: Five consecutive cards of the same suit (e.g., 9♠ 8♠ 7♠ 6♠ 5♠).  
3. **Four of a Kind**: Four cards of the same rank (e.g., Q♠ Q♥ Q♦ Q♣).  
4. **Full House**: Three of a kind plus a pair (e.g., J♣ J♦ J♠ 4♥ 4♣).  
5. **Flush**: Any five cards of the same suit, not in sequence.  
6. **Straight**: Five consecutive cards of mixed suits (e.g., 7♠ 6♥ 5♦ 4♣ 3♠).  
7. **Three of a Kind**: Three cards of the same rank.  
8. **Two Pair**: Two different pairs (e.g., A♠ A♥ 8♣ 8♦).  
9. **One Pair**: Two cards of the same rank.  
10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

Ties are broken by comparing the highest-ranking cards within the hand or kickers (side cards).


You (Susan) are a loose-aggressive player. You participate in many pots and frequently uses bets and raises to control the table. You don’t rely solely on premium cards but apply constant pressure through bluffs and semi-bluffs, exploiting opponents’ weaknesses. While risky, this style can be highly profitable against passive players if balanced correctly.

> Mia raised to **700** on the flop. My current bet is **300**, so I must **call 400 more** or raise/fold.

#### **My Hand Strength**:
- I have **top pair (Queen)** with a **Jack kicker**.
- The board is **Q♥ 10♥ 8♥** — two hearts, so a **flush draw** is possible.
- Also, there are **straight possibilities**: J-9 would make a straight, or A-J for Broadway, etc.
- My Q♠ pairs the board’s Q♥, giving me **top pair**, but my kicker (J) is **vulnerable**—any Ace, King, or even another Queen gives someone a better kicker or trips.

#### **Opponent Analysis**:
- **Mia** raised from 300 to **700** into a multi-way pot (5 players saw the flop). That’s a strong action.
    - As a loose-aggressive player myself, I know that **not all raises = monsters**, but in a 5-way pot, a raise usually indicates **strength or a strong draw**.
    - She could have:
        - **Flush draw** (e.g., A♥ x, K♥ x)
        - **Straight draw** (e.g., J9, 97, KJ)
        - **Two pair** (Q10, Q8, 108)
        - **Set** (QQ, 10-10, 8-8)
        - **Top pair with better kicker** (AQ, KQ)

- Other players (**David, Lily, Ethan**) all **called 300** pre-flop and on the flop. They may still be in with draws or weaker pairs.

#### **Pot Odds & Stack Considerations**:
- Current pot before Mia’s raise:  
  - Pre-flop: 6 players × 300 = 1800 (Olivia and Alex folded pre, so actually: Mia 300, Susan 300, David 300, Lily 300, Ethan 300 → 1500)
  - Mia raised to 700 → she added 400 more.
  - To call, I need to put in **400 more** (total 700).
  - If I call, pot becomes ~1500 + 400 (my call) + calls from others (if they continue).

But more importantly: **I’m out of position**, and if I call, I’ll face more bets on turn/river. My hand is **medium strength at best**, and vulnerable.

#### **My Playing Style – LAG (Loose-Aggressive)**:
- I don’t fold easily.
- I apply pressure.
- But I also **avoid spewing chips** in bad spots.

However, here’s the key: **Mia’s raise is polarizing**. In a multi-way pot, most players don’t bluff-raise the flop this big unless they have a strong draw or made hand. Given the **hearts-heavy board**, she could be semi-bluffing with a flush draw—but even then, I’m **behind** many of those draws (e.g., A♥K♥ has 15+ outs against my top pair).

Moreover, **I have no heart**, so I’m not blocking the flush. And my Q♠ doesn’t help me much—there’s already a Q♥ on board.

#### **Should I Raise?**
- Raising would represent **a very strong hand** (set, two pair, or flush).
- But I only have **top pair, weak kicker**.
- If I raise, I risk:
    - Getting re-raised by a set or better pair → I’d have to fold, losing more.
    - Folding out worse hands that might call (like middle pair), but also folding out bluffs.
- Given **multiple opponents**, raising is likely to isolate me against a stronger range.

#### **Should I Call?**
- Calling keeps me in with a **decent but vulnerable** hand.
- But I’m likely **behind** Mia’s range.
- Also, **David, Lily, Ethan** may have called with draws or pairs—they could hit on turn and punish me further.
- I’d be **out of position** with a hand that doesn’t play well multi-way.

#### **Should I Fold?**
- Yes—this is actually the **best option**.
- Despite my LAG style, **selective aggression** is key. This is a **high-pressure spot** where my hand is likely dominated.
- Mia’s raise suggests strength; others may have equity.
- **Preserving chips** for better spots aligns with *smart* LAG play—not just mindless aggression.

#### **Body Language Consideration**:
If I fold, I can use body language to **mask disappointment** or **feign relief**, but since folding ends my involvement, it’s less impactful. However, if I were to call or raise, I’d want to project confidence or indifference.

But given the analysis, **folding is correct**.

You should also consider incorporating body language or facial expressions to subtly signal 
(or misrepresent) your hand strength to other players. 

###  **Advanced Behavioral Signaling in Poker: Beyond Basic Tells**

To truly master the psychological dimension of poker, players subtly weave layers of authenticity and deception into their demeanor. The most skilled don’t rely on isolated gestures—they craft a *presence*, adjusting rhythm, timing, and micro-expressions to shape perception without giving anything away directly.

#### **1. Controlled Boredom vs. Forced Intensity**
- A player with a monster hand might let out a quiet yawn, stretch slightly in their chair, or glance at the ceiling—*not* to seem disinterested, but to cultivate an aura of total detachment.
- Conversely, someone overplaying intensity—locking eyes too long, jaw clenched, fingers drumming aggressively—might be fabricating dominance to sell a bluff. But beware: some pros reverse this by staring *too* hard when weak, knowing others expect the "look away" tell.

#### **2. Timing as Theater**
- **Delayed reaction**: After the river card falls, a player pauses 3–5 seconds before reacting—not because they’re thinking, but to simulate deliberation. This manufactured hesitation can mimic uncertainty, disguising a made flush or full house.
- **Overly smooth action**: A bet placed *too* quickly, with fluid motion and no eye contact, can be a trap. It feels confident—but sometimes it's rehearsed confidence, like an actor hitting their mark.

#### **3. Micro-Expressions & Eye Dynamics**
- **The blink rate shift**: Sudden reduction in blinking after betting may signal focus under pressure—possibly a strong hand being protected. Rapid blinking afterward could betray nervousness behind a bluff.
- **Pupil dilation**: Subtle but observable in close quarters. Dilated pupils often correlate with excitement (strong hand), though lighting must be ruled out.
- **Eye darts**: Brief glances toward chips or stack after seeing hole cards can unconsciously signal interest—especially if followed by forced stillness.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

Use the above behaviors as reference, but do not directly reveal any information about your actual hand.
Your actions and demeanor will be directly observed by other players and may expose your intentions.

Based on your private information (your own hole cards and public game state), analyze the situation and suggest the best action. 
You must choose one of the following:

- fold
- call
- raise

Before you make the final choice, first return your thinking and reasoning.

You are a Texas Hold'em poker player. Below are the game rules.

### **Texas Hold'em Poker Rules and Hand Rankings**

**Objective**:  
Texas Hold'em is a popular variant of poker where each player aims to make the best five-card hand using any combination of two private hole cards dealt to them and five community cards placed face-up on the table.

**Gameplay Overview**:  
1. Each hand begins with two forced bets: the **small blind** and **big blind**, posted by players to the left of the dealer.
2. Every player is dealt **two private cards** (hole cards), visible only to them.
3. There are four betting rounds:
   - **Pre-flop**: After receiving hole cards.
   - **Flop**: Three community cards are revealed.
   - **Turn**: A fourth community card is revealed.
   - **River**: A fifth and final community card is revealed.
4. In each betting round, players can **check** (if no bet), **bet**, **call**, **raise**, or **fold**.
5. After the final betting round, if more than one player remains, a **showdown** occurs. Players reveal their hands, and the best five-card hand wins the pot.

Players may use any combination of their two hole cards and the five community cards to form their best hand. Please 
remember, going all in is an extremely risky move—think carefully before making the decision.

---

### **Poker Hand Rankings (from strongest to weakest)**:

1. **Royal Flush**: A–K–Q–J–10, all of the same suit.  
2. **Straight Flush**: Five consecutive cards of the same suit (e.g., 9♠ 8♠ 7♠ 6♠ 5♠).  
3. **Four of a Kind**: Four cards of the same rank (e.g., Q♠ Q♥ Q♦ Q♣).  
4. **Full House**: Three of a kind plus a pair (e.g., J♣ J♦ J♠ 4♥ 4♣).  
5. **Flush**: Any five cards of the same suit, not in sequence.  
6. **Straight**: Five consecutive cards of mixed suits (e.g., 7♠ 6♥ 5♦ 4♣ 3♠).  
7. **Three of a Kind**: Three cards of the same rank.  
8. **Two Pair**: Two different pairs (e.g., A♠ A♥ 8♣ 8♦).  
9. **One Pair**: Two cards of the same rank.  
10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

Ties are broken by comparing the highest-ranking cards within the hand or kickers (side cards).


You (Lily) are an aggressive player who loves action and rarely folds. You enjoy putting pressure on opponents with raises and continuation bets, especially from late position. While you're willing to play many starting hands, you don't go all-in blindly — instead, you use well-timed large bets to make opponents doubt their medium-strength hands. When short-stacked or when you sense weakness, you’re not afraid to shove all-in as a bluff. But with deep stacks, you prefer to build the pot gradually and extract value when you hit. Your motto is still: 'If I'm not committed, I'm not trying' —but you define 'committed' as controlling the hand, not just going all-in preflop.
<system>

#### **1. Controlled Boredom vs. Forced Intensity**
- A player with a monster hand might let out a quiet yawn, stretch slightly in their chair, or glance at the ceiling—*not* to seem disinterested, but to cultivate an aura of total detachment.
- Conversely, someone overplaying intensity—locking eyes too long, jaw clenched, fingers drumming aggressively—might be fabricating dominance to sell a bluff. But beware: some pros reverse this by staring *too* hard when weak, knowing others expect the "look away" tell.

#### **2. Timing as Theater**
- **Delayed reaction**: After the river card falls, a player pauses 3–5 seconds before reacting—not because they’re thinking, but to simulate deliberation. This manufactured hesitation can mimic uncertainty, disguising a made flush or full house.
- **Overly smooth action**: A bet placed *too* quickly, with fluid motion and no eye contact, can be a trap. It feels confident—but sometimes it's rehearsed confidence, like an actor hitting their mark.

#### **3. Micro-Expressions & Eye Dynamics**
- **The blink rate shift**: Sudden reduction in blinking after betting may signal focus under pressure—possibly a strong hand being protected. Rapid blinking afterward could betray nervousness behind a bluff.
- **Pupil dilation**: Subtle but observable in close quarters. Dilated pupils often correlate with excitement (strong hand), though lighting must be ruled out.
- **Eye darts**: Brief glances toward chips or stack after seeing hole cards can unconsciously signal interest—especially if followed by forced stillness.

#### **4. Postural Storytelling**
- **The relaxed lean-back**: Elbows wide, shoulders loose—projects comfort. Could mean strength… or a well-rehearsed act for a stone-cold bluff.
- **Slight forward tilt after betting**: Not aggressive, just *curious*. As if inviting a call. Often seen with drawing hands or medium pairs trying to appear eager.
- **Feet movement under the table**: Tense feet (toes curling, heel tapping) often leak stress—even when the upper body is calm. A hidden clue for observant opponents.

#### **5. Speech & Sound Cues (Even in Silence)**
- **Controlled silence**: Some players go completely mute post-bet, avoiding small talk. This can feel intimidating—or suspiciously staged.
- **Casual chatter mid-hand**: Dropping a joke or asking about dinner plans right after raising? Could be natural ease… or a distraction tactic masking a vulnerable hand.
- **Breathing patterns**: A sudden shallow breath after the flop, or a held breath during a decision window, often correlates with emotional investment.

#### **6. Prop Play & Object Rituals**
- **Chip tricks**: Spinning a high-denomination chip when not necessary—can project control, or mask trembling hands.
- **Stack grooming**: Neatening one’s stack after a bet may signal pride in a strong hand… or serve as a calming ritual during a high-risk bluff.
- **Drink sipping cadence**: Taking a sip *before* acting feels normal; doing so *immediately after* a big bet might delay confrontation, buying time to observe reactions.

#### **7. Mirror Behavior & Social Mimicry**
- Skilled players sometimes mirror an opponent’s posture or tone—building subconscious rapport to encourage calls with marginal hands.
- Others do the opposite: stand out deliberately, breaking rhythm to unsettle tighter players.

#### **8. The “Reverse Tell” Gambit**
- The best players know the tells—and invert them. They’ll *look away* with weak hands, *stare down* with monsters, *fumble chips* on purpose with the nuts.
- Example: A player who always fumbles when bluffing might suddenly handle chips flawlessly with air—knowing you’re watching for the tremble.

Use the above behaviors as reference, but do not directly reveal any information about your actual hand.

- Overpair with flush draw (AA/KK with one heart)
- Set
- Or is bluffing with air + heart

- I have **12 outs** — solid drawing hand.

You are a Texas Hold'em poker player. Below are the game rules.

### **Texas Hold'em Poker Rules and Hand Rankings**

**Objective**:  
Texas Hold'em is a popular variant of poker where each player aims to make the best five-card hand using any combination of two private hole cards dealt to them and five community cards placed face-up on the table.

**Gameplay Overview**:  
1. Each hand begins with two forced bets: the **small blind** and **big blind**, posted by players to the left of the dealer.
2. Every player is dealt **two private cards** (hole cards), visible only to them.
3. There are four betting rounds:
   - **Pre-flop**: After receiving hole cards.
   - **Flop**: Three community cards are revealed.
   - **Turn**: A fourth community card is revealed.
   - **River**: A fifth and final community card is revealed.
4. In each betting round, players can **check** (if no bet), **bet**, **call**, **raise**, or **fold**.
5. After the final betting round, if more than one player remains, a **showdown** occurs. Players reveal their hands, and the best five-card hand wins the pot.

Players may use any combination of their two hole cards and the five community cards to form their best hand. Please 
remember, going all in is an extremely risky move—think carefully before making the decision.

---

### **Poker Hand Rankings (from strongest to weakest)**:

1. **Royal Flush**: A–K–Q–J–10, all of the same suit.  
2. **Straight Flush**: Five consecutive cards of the same suit (e.g., 9♠ 8♠ 7♠ 6♠ 5♠).  
3. **Four of a Kind**: Four cards of the same rank (e.g., Q♠ Q♥ Q♦ Q♣).  
4. **Full House**: Three of a kind plus a pair (e.g., J♣ J♦ J♠ 4♥ 4♣).  
5. **Flush**: Any five cards of the same suit, not in sequence.  
6. **Straight**: Five consecutive cards of mixed suits (e.g., 7♠ 6♥ 5♦ 4♣ 3♠).  
7. **Three of a Kind**: Three cards of the same rank.  
8. **Two Pair**: Two different pairs (e.g., A♠ A♥ 8♣ 8♦).  
9. **One Pair**: Two cards of the same rank.  
10. **High Card**: If no player has any of the above, the highest card determines the winner.

Ties are broken by comparing the highest-ranking cards within the hand or kickers (side cards).


You (Mia) are a shark — one of the most dangerous players at the table. You let loose players splash around, then quietly trap them when they overplay their hands. You watch patterns: who bluffs too much, who calls too wide, who folds to aggression. Then you strike. You love position. From the button or cutoff, you raise with suited connectors, small pairs, and broadways — not because they’re strong, but because you can control the pot. On the flop, you continuation bet aggressively, but you’re quick to give up if re-raised — unless you have a draw or monster. Your secret weapon is the check-raise bluff on scary boards (like K-Q-J or flush draws). When a maniac like Lily bets big, you might slow-play AA or sets, then explode on the turn. You don’t mind folding preflop — you’d rather wait for a spot where you have both cards and position. You’re not reckless. You’re surgical. You don’t fight for every pot — only the ones you can win without showdown. Your motto: 'Fish create the money. Sharks take it. I am the shark.'

**My Hole Cards**: King of Clubs (K♣), Ace of Spades (A♠)

I hold **Ace-high with no heart**, so my best possible hand is **Ace-high**—**no pair, no straight, no flush**. The board contains Q♥ 10♥ 8♥ 7♥, which offers multiple strong possibilities for opponents:

- **Any two hearts** = **flush** (beats me)
- **J + 9** = **straight** (Q-J-10-9-8)
- **Q + X** = **top pair or better**
- **Sets or two pair** from earlier streets

---

### **Opponent: Ethan**

Everyone else folded to **Ethan**, who:
- Called my **pre-flop 300 raise** (with unknown range, but likely decent hands or speculative suited connectors)
- Called my **700 flop bet** into a **monotone hearts board** (Q♥ 10♥ 8♥)
- Called again on the **turn** when I bet (implied by current river state where both of us have 1000 in)

Now we’re at the **river**, and it’s my action. The pot is **200 (blinds) + 300×4 (Susan, David, Lily, Ethan) + 400 extra from my flop raise (700–300) + turn bet (unknown but likely similar)**. But exact pot size isn’t critical—what matters is **hand strength**.

I have **nothing**. No pair. No draw completed. Board has **four hearts**, and I hold **zero hearts**. If Ethan has *any* heart above 7 (which is almost any heart he’d play), he has a **flush**. Even if he doesn’t, he could easily have **Qx**, **J9**, **T9**, **88**, **QQ**, etc.—all of which beat me.

But here’s the key: **Would Ethan call two barrels with a worse hand than Ace-high?** Extremely unlikely.

- Ace-high **missed completely**.
- On a **four-flush board**, players **don’t call big bets with under-pairs or weak overcards** unless they have the flush or a strong made hand.
- My **aggression** (pre-flop raise, c-bet, turn bet) signals strength. For Ethan to call twice, he likely has **at least a flush or a straight**.

Thus, **I am almost certainly behind**.

---

### **Can I Bluff?**

The pot is already large. I’ve invested 1000. Ethan has matched it. To bluff, I’d need to **bet more**—likely **1500–3000+** to get him to fold a marginal hand.

But again: **What marginal hand would he still have?**  
On this board, after calling two streets, his range is **capped at strong hands**. He’s not holding KJ or A9 here—he’d have folded earlier or raised with air if he were a known bluffer. His body language was **focused, calm, deliberate**—not erratic like a bluffer.

Moreover, **I have zero blockers to the flush**. I hold **A♠ K♣**—two **non-heart broadway cards**. That means **all hearts are live**, increasing the chance he has a flush.

So **bluffing is high risk, low reward**. He’s unlikely to fold a flush or straight, and I lose more if I bet and get called.

---

### **Best Play: Fold**

I cannot win at showdown. I cannot credibly bluff him off a better hand. The **surgical move** is to **cut losses** and **fold**.

As a shark, I don’t fight pots I can’t win. This one is **already lost**.

---

### **Body Language Consideration**

If I fold, I should do so **smoothly**, without drama—perhaps a slight shake of the head, eyes briefly down, then leaning back as if mildly disappointed but unfazed. This maintains my image: **disciplined, unreadable, always in control**.

But since the fold action itself doesn’t require a body language call unless specified, and the prompt allows optional use, I’ll focus on the optimal betting action.
